(February&#160;9, 2026<span class="noprint">&#59;&#32;5 days ago</span><span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">2026-02-09</span>)</span><sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" i860</a>, <a href="/wiki/DEC_Alpha" title="DEC Alpha">DEC Alpha</a>, <a href="/wiki/Itanium" title="Itanium">Itanium</a>, <a href="/wiki/MIPS_architecture" title="MIPS architecture">MIPS</a>, and <a href="/wiki/RSX-11" title="RSX-11">RSX-11</a>, <a href="/wiki/VAXELN" title="VAXELN">VAXELN</a>, <a href="/wiki/OpenVMS" title="OpenVMS">OpenVMS</a>, <a href="/wiki/DEC_MICA" title="DEC MICA">MICA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mach_(kernel)" title="Mach (kernel)">Mach</a> (kernel)<br /> <a href="/wiki/MS-DOS" title="MS-DOS">MS-DOS</a>, <a href="/wiki/OS/2" title="OS/2">OS/2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windows_3.1" title="Windows 3.1">Windows 3.1</a> (userland)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space: nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/User_interface" title="User class="infobox-data">Depending on version, edition or customer choice: <a href="/wiki/Trialware" class="mw-redirect" title="Trialware">Trialware</a>, <a href="/wiki/Commercial_software" title="Commercial software">commercial software</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volume_licensing" title="Volume licensing">volume licensing</a>, <a href="/wiki/OEM" class="mw-redirect" title="OEM">OEM</a>-only, <a href="/wiki/SaaS" class="mw-redirect" title="SaaS">SaaS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Software_plus_services" class="mw-redirect" title="Software plus services">S+S</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>a<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space: nowrap;">Official website</th><td class="infobox-data"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://windows.com">windows<wbr />.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Windows NT</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Proprietary_software" title="Proprietary software">proprietary</a> <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">graphical</a> <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a> produced by <a href="/wiki/Microsoft" title="Microsoft">Microsoft</a> as part of its <a href="/wiki/Windows" class="mw-redirect" title="Windows">Windows</a> product line, the first version of which, <a href="/wiki/Windows_NT_3.1" title="Windows NT 3.1">Windows NT 3.1</a>, was released on July 27, 1993. Originally made for the <a href="/wiki/Workstation" title="Workstation">workstation</a>, office, and <a href="/wiki/Server_(computing)" title="Server (computing)">server</a> markets, the Windows NT line was made available to <a href="/wiki/Consumer" title="Consumer">consumers</a> with the release of <a href="/wiki/Windows_XP" title="Windows XP">Windows XP</a> in 2001. The underlying technology of Windows NT continues to exist to this day with incremental changes and improvements, with the latest version of Windows based on Windows NT being <a href="/wiki/Windows_Server_2025" title="Windows Server 2025">Windows Server 2025</a> announced in 2024.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The name "Windows NT" originally denoted the major technological advancements that it had introduced to the Windows product line, including eliminating the <a href="/wiki/16-bit_computing" title="16-bit computing">16-bit memory access</a> limitations of earlier Windows releases such as <a href="/wiki/Windows_3.1" title="Windows 3.1">Windows 3.1</a>. Each Windows release built on this technology is considered to be based on, if not a revision of Windows NT, even though the Windows NT name itself has not been used in any other Windows releases since <a href="/wiki/Windows_NT_4.0" title="Windows NT 4.0">Windows NT 4.0</a> in 1996.
</p><p>Windows NT provides many more features than other Windows releases, among them being support for <a href="/wiki/Multiprocessing" title="Multiprocessing">multiprocessing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Multi-user_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Multi-user systems">multi-user systems</a>, a "pure" <a href="/wiki/32-bit" class="mw-redirect" title="32-bit">32-bit</a> kernel with 32-bit memory addressing, support for <a href="/wiki/Instruction_set_architecture" title="Instruction set architecture">instruction sets</a> other than <a href="/wiki/X86" title="X86">x86</a>, and many other system services such as <a href="/wiki/Active_Directory" title="Active Directory">Active Directory</a> and more. Newer versions of Windows NT support <a href="/wiki/64-bit_computing" title="64-bit computing">64-bit computing</a>, with a 64-bit kernel and 64-bit memory addressing.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Product_line">Product line</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Windows_NT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Product line"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Windows NT is a group or family of products—like Windows is a group or family. Windows NT is a sub-grouping of Windows.
</p><p>The first version of Windows NT, <a href="/wiki/Windows_NT_3.1" title="Windows NT 3.1">3.1</a>, was produced for <a href="/wiki/Workstation" title="Workstation">workstation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Server_computer" class="mw-redirect" title="Server computer">server</a> computers. It was commercially focused—and intended to complement consumer versions of Windows that were based on <a href="/wiki/MS-DOS" title="MS-DOS">MS-DOS</a> (including <a href="/wiki/Windows_1.0" title="Windows 1.0">Windows 1.0</a> through <a href="/wiki/Windows_3.1" title="Windows 3.1">Windows 3.1x</a>). In 1996, <a href="/wiki/Windows_NT_4.0" title="Windows NT 4.0">Windows NT 4.0</a> was released, including the new shell from <a href="/wiki/Windows_95" title="Windows 95">Windows 95</a>.
</p><p>Eventually, Microsoft incorporated the Windows NT technology into the Windows product line for <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">personal</a> computing and deprecated the <a href="/wiki/Windows_9x" title="Windows 9x">Windows 9x</a> family. Starting with <a href="/wiki/Windows_2000" title="Windows 2000">Windows 2000</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nt5.0&#95;is&#95;windows&#95;2000&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nt5.0_is_windows_2000-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "NT" was removed from the product name yet is still in several low-level places in the system—including for a while as part of the product version.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Installing">Installing</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Windows_NT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Installing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Versions of Windows NT are installed using <a href="/wiki/Windows_Setup#Windows_NT" title="Windows Setup">Windows Setup</a>, which, starting with <a href="/wiki/Windows_Vista" title="Windows Vista">Windows Vista</a>, uses the <a href="/wiki/Windows_Preinstallation_Environment" title="Windows Preinstallation Environment">Windows Preinstallation Environment</a>, which is a lightweight version of Windows NT made for deployment of the operating system.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Since Windows Vista, the Windows installation files, as well as the preinstallation environment used to install Windows, are stored in the <a href="/wiki/Windows_Imaging_Format" title="Windows Imaging Format">Windows Imaging Format</a>. It is possible to use the <a href="/wiki/Deployment_Image_Servicing_and_Management" class="mw-redirect" title="Deployment Image Servicing and Management">Deployment Image Servicing and Management</a> (DISM) tool to install Windows from the command line and skip the GUI installer.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Naming">Naming</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Windows_NT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Naming"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>It has been suggested that <a href="/wiki/Dave_Cutler" title="Dave Cutler">Dave Cutler</a> intended the initialism "WNT" as a play on <a href="/wiki/OpenVMS" title="OpenVMS">VMS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caesar_cipher" title="Caesar cipher">incrementing each letter by one</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-zachary&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zachary-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the project was originally intended as a follow-on to <a href="/wiki/OS/2" title="OS/2">OS/2</a> and was referred to as "NT OS/2" before receiving the Windows brand.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Two of the original NT developers, <a href="/wiki/Mark_Lucovsky" title="Mark Lucovsky">Mark Lucovsky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dave_Plummer" title="Dave Plummer">Dave W. Plummer</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-x&#95;davepl&#95;says&#95;N10&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-x_davepl_says_N10-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> state that the name was taken from the original target processor—the <a href="/wiki/Intel_i860" title="Intel i860">Intel i860</a>, code-named N10 ("N-Ten").<sup id="cite&#95;ref-thurrott-roadtogold&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thurrott-roadtogold-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 1991 video featuring <a href="/wiki/Bill_Gates" title="Bill Gates">Bill Gates</a> and Microsoft products specifically says that "Windows NT stands for 'New Technology<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Seven years later in 1998, during a question-and-answer (Q&amp;A) session, he then revealed that the letters were previously <a href="/wiki/Backronym" title="Backronym">expanded</a> to such but no longer carry any specific meaning.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The letters were dropped from the names of releases from Windows 2000 and later, though Microsoft described that product as being "Built on NT Technology".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nt5.0&#95;is&#95;windows&#95;2000&#95;12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nt5.0_is_windows_2000-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>"NT" was a trademark of Northern Telecom (later <a href="/wiki/Nortel" title="Nortel">Nortel</a>), which Microsoft was forced to acknowledge on the product packaging.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Major_features">Major features</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Windows_NT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Major features"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>One of the main purposes of NT is hardware and software portability. Various versions of NT family operating systems have been released for a variety of processor architectures, initially <a href="/wiki/IA-32" title="IA-32">IA-32</a>, <a href="/wiki/MIPS_architecture" title="MIPS architecture">MIPS</a>, and <a href="/wiki/DEC_Alpha" title="DEC Alpha">DEC Alpha</a>, with <a href="/wiki/PowerPC" title="PowerPC">PowerPC</a>, <a href="/wiki/Itanium" title="Itanium">Itanium</a>, <a href="/wiki/X86-64" title="X86-64">x86-64</a> and <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="ARM architecture">ARM</a> supported in later releases. An initial idea was to have a common code base with a custom <a href="/wiki/Hardware_Abstraction_Layer" class="mw-redirect" title="Hardware Abstraction Layer">Hardware Abstraction Layer</a> (HAL) for each platform. However, support for MIPS, Alpha, and PowerPC was later dropped in <a href="/wiki/Windows_2000" title="Windows 2000">Windows 2000</a>. Broad software compatibility was initially achieved with support for several <a href="/wiki/API" title="API">API</a> "personalities", including <a href="/wiki/Windows_API" title="Windows API">Windows API</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_POSIX_subsystem" title="Microsoft POSIX subsystem">POSIX</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/OS/2" title="OS/2">OS/2</a> APIs<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>—the latter two were phased out starting with Windows XP.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Partial <a href="/wiki/MS-DOS" title="MS-DOS">MS-DOS</a> and Windows 16-bit compatibility is achieved on IA-32 via an integrated <a href="/wiki/NTVDM" class="mw-redirect" title="NTVDM">DOS Virtual Machine</a>—although this feature is not available on other architectures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>NT has supported per-object (file, function, and role) <a href="/wiki/Access_control_list" class="mw-redirect" title="Access control list">access control lists</a> allowing a rich set of security permissions to be applied to systems and services. NT
has also supported Windows network protocols, inheriting the previous OS/2 <a href="/wiki/LAN_Manager" title="LAN Manager">LAN Manager</a> networking, as well as <a href="/wiki/TCP/IP" class="mw-redirect" title="TCP/IP">TCP/IP</a> networking (for which Microsoft used to implement a TCP/IP stack derived at first from a <a href="/wiki/STREAMS" title="STREAMS">STREAMS</a>-based stack from <a href="/wiki/Spider_Systems" title="Spider Systems">Spider Systems</a>, then later rewritten in-house).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Windows NT 3.1 was the first version of Windows to use 32-bit flat virtual memory addressing on 32-bit processors. Its companion product, Windows 3.1, used segmented addressing and switches from 16-bit to 32-bit addressing in pages.
</p><p>Windows NT 3.1 featured a core kernel providing a system API, running in <a href="/wiki/Supervisor_mode" class="mw-redirect" title="Supervisor mode">supervisor mode</a> (ring 0 in x86; referred to in Windows NT as "kernel mode" on all platforms), and a set of user-space environments with their own APIs which included the new Win32 environment, an OS/2 1.3 text-mode environment and a POSIX environment. The full <a href="/wiki/Preemptive_multitasking" class="mw-redirect" title="Preemptive multitasking">preemptive multitasking</a> kernel could interrupt running tasks to <a href="/wiki/Windows_NT_Processor_scheduling" class="mw-redirect" title="Windows NT Processor scheduling">schedule</a> other tasks, without relying on user programs to voluntarily give up control of the CPU, as in Windows 3.1 Windows applications (although MS-DOS applications were preemptively multitasked in Windows starting with <a href="/wiki/Windows_2.0x#Release_versions" class="mw-redirect" title="Windows 2.0x">Windows/386</a>).
</p><p>Notably, in Windows NT 3.x, several I/O driver subsystems, such as video and printing, were <a href="/wiki/User_space" class="mw-redirect" title="User space">user-mode</a> subsystems. In Windows NT 4.0, the video, server, and printer spooler subsystems were moved into kernel mode. Windows NT's first <a href="/wiki/GUI" class="mw-redirect" title="GUI">GUI</a> was strongly influenced by (and programmatically compatible with) that from Windows 3.1; Windows NT 4.0's interface was redesigned to match that of the brand-new <a href="/wiki/Windows_95" title="Windows 95">Windows 95</a>, moving from the <a href="/wiki/Program_Manager" title="Program Manager">Program Manager</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Windows_shell" title="Windows shell">Windows shell</a> design.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/NTFS" title="NTFS">NTFS</a>, a journaled, secure file system, is a major feature of NT. Windows NT also allows for other installable file systems; NT can also be installed on <a href="/wiki/File_Allocation_Table" title="File Allocation Table">FAT</a> file systems, and versions 3.1, 3.5, and 3.51 could be installed on <a href="/wiki/High_Performance_File_System" title="High Performance File System">HPFS</a> file systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Windows NT introduced its own driver model, the Windows NT driver model, and is incompatible with older driver frameworks. With <a href="/wiki/Windows_2000" title="Windows 2000">Windows 2000</a>, the Windows NT driver model was enhanced to become the <a href="/wiki/Windows_Driver_Model" title="Windows Driver Model">Windows Driver Model</a>, which was first introduced with <a href="/wiki/Windows_98" title="Windows 98">Windows 98</a>, but was based on the NT driver model.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Windows_Vista" title="Windows Vista">Windows Vista</a> added native support for the <a href="/wiki/Windows_Driver_Foundation" class="mw-redirect" title="Windows Driver Foundation">Windows Driver Foundation</a>, which is also available for <a href="/wiki/Windows_XP" title="Windows XP">Windows XP</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windows_Server_2003" title="Windows Server 2003">Windows Server 2003</a> and to an extent, <a href="/wiki/Windows_2000" title="Windows 2000">Windows 2000</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Development">Development</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Windows_NT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Development"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Windows_NT_logo.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Windows_NT_logo.svg/250px-Windows_NT_logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="49" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Windows_NT_logo.svg/500px-Windows_NT_logo.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="4722" data-file-height="920" /></a><figcaption>Windows NT wordmark for <a href="/wiki/Windows_NT_4.0" title="Windows NT 4.0">version 4.0</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>Microsoft decided to create a portable operating system, compatible with <a href="/wiki/OS/2" title="OS/2">OS/2</a> and <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_POSIX_subsystem" title="Microsoft POSIX subsystem">POSIX</a> and supporting <a href="/wiki/Multiprocessing" title="Multiprocessing">multiprocessing</a>, in October 1988.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When development started in November 1989, Windows NT was to be known as <a href="/wiki/OS/2" title="OS/2">OS/2</a> 3.0,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the third version of the operating system developed jointly by Microsoft and <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>. To ensure portability, initial development was targeted at the <a href="/wiki/Intel_i860" title="Intel i860">Intel i860</a>XR <a href="/wiki/RISC_processor" class="mw-redirect" title="RISC processor">RISC processor</a>, switching to the <a href="/wiki/MIPS_architecture" title="MIPS architecture">MIPS</a> <a href="/wiki/R3000" title="R3000">R3000</a> in late 1989, and then the <a href="/wiki/Intel_i386" class="mw-redirect" title="Intel i386">Intel i386</a> in 1990.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-thurrott-roadtogold&#95;17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thurrott-roadtogold-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Microsoft also continued parallel development of the DOS-based and less <a href="/wiki/Resource_(computer_science)" class="mw-redirect" title="Resource (computer science)">resource</a>-demanding Windows environment, resulting in the release of <a href="/wiki/Windows_3.0" title="Windows 3.0">Windows 3.0</a> in May 1990.
</p><p>Windows 3.0 was eventually so successful that Microsoft decided to change the primary <a href="/wiki/Application_programming_interface" class="mw-redirect" title="Application programming interface">application programming interface</a> for the still unreleased NT OS/2 (as it was then known) from an extended OS/2 API to an extended <a href="/wiki/Windows_API" title="Windows API">Windows API</a>. This decision caused tension between Microsoft and IBM and the collaboration ultimately fell apart.
</p><p>IBM continued OS/2 development alone while Microsoft continued work on the newly renamed Windows NT. Though neither operating system would immediately be as popular as Microsoft's MS-DOS or Windows products, Windows NT would eventually be far more successful than OS/2.
</p><p>Microsoft hired a group of developers from <a href="/wiki/Digital_Equipment_Corporation" title="Digital Equipment Corporation">Digital Equipment Corporation</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Dave_Cutler" title="Dave Cutler">Dave Cutler</a> to build Windows NT, and many elements of the design reflect earlier DEC experience with Cutler's <a href="/wiki/OpenVMS" title="OpenVMS">VMS</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-russinovich19981130&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-russinovich19981130-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/VAXELN" title="VAXELN">VAXELN</a> and <a href="/wiki/RSX-11" title="RSX-11">RSX-11</a>, but also an unreleased object-based operating system developed by Cutler at Digital codenamed <a href="/wiki/DEC_MICA" title="DEC MICA">MICA</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-foreword&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-foreword-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The team was joined by selected members of the disbanded OS/2 team, including <a href="/wiki/Moshe_Dunie" title="Moshe Dunie">Moshe Dunie</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-zachary&#95;14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zachary-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Windows_2000_architecture.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Windows_2000_architecture.svg/250px-Windows_2000_architecture.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="321" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Windows_2000_architecture.svg/500px-Windows_2000_architecture.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="663" data-file-height="850" /></a><figcaption>Windows 2000 architecture</figcaption></figure>
<p>Although NT was not an exact clone of Cutler's previous operating systems, DEC engineers almost immediately noticed the internal similarities. Parts of <i>VAX/VMS Internals and Data Structures</i>, published by <a href="/wiki/Digital_Press" class="mw-redirect" title="Digital Press">Digital Press</a>, accurately describe Windows NT internals using VMS terms. Furthermore, parts of the NT codebase's directory structure and filenames matched that of the MICA codebase.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-zachary&#95;14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zachary-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Instead of a lawsuit, Microsoft agreed to pay DEC $65–100&#160;million, help market VMS, train Digital personnel on Windows NT, and continue Windows NT support for the DEC Alpha.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-russinovich19981130&#95;30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-russinovich19981130-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Windows NT and VMS <a href="/wiki/Memory_management" title="Memory management">memory management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Process_(computing)" title="Process (computing)">processes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scheduling_(computing)" title="Scheduling (computing)">scheduling</a> are very similar. Windows NT's <a href="/wiki/Process_management_(computing)" title="Process management (computing)">process management</a> differs by implementing <a href="/wiki/Thread_(computing)" title="Thread (computing)">threading</a>, which DEC did not implement until VMS 7.0 in 1995.
</p><p>Like VMS,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-russinovich19981130&#95;30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-russinovich19981130-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Windows NT's kernel mode code distinguishes between the "kernel", whose primary purpose is to implement processor- and architecture-dependent functions, and the "executive". This was designed as a modified <a href="/wiki/Microkernel" title="Microkernel">microkernel</a>, as the Windows NT kernel was influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Mach_microkernel" class="mw-redirect" title="Mach microkernel">Mach microkernel</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Rashid" title="Richard Rashid">Richard Rashid</a> at Carnegie Mellon University,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but does not meet all of the criteria of a pure microkernel. Both the kernel and the executive are <a href="/wiki/Linker_(computing)" title="Linker (computing)">linked</a> together into the single loaded module <a href="/wiki/Ntoskrnl.exe" title="Ntoskrnl.exe">ntoskrnl.exe</a>; from outside this module, there is little distinction between the kernel and the executive. Routines from each are directly accessible, as for example from kernel-mode device drivers.
</p><p>API sets in the Windows NT family are implemented as subsystems atop the publicly undocumented <a href="/wiki/Native_API" class="mw-redirect" title="Native API">"native" API</a>; this allowed the late adoption of the Windows API (into the Win32 subsystem). Windows NT was one of the earliest operating systems to use <a href="/wiki/UCS-2" class="mw-redirect" title="UCS-2">UCS-2</a> and <a href="/wiki/UTF-16" title="UTF-16">UTF-16</a> internally.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Architecture">Architecture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Windows_NT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Architecture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Windows_NT" title="Architecture of Windows NT">Architecture of Windows NT</a></div>
<p>Windows NT uses a layered design <a href="/wiki/Software_architecture" title="Software architecture">architecture</a> that consists of two main components, <a href="/wiki/User_mode" class="mw-redirect" title="User mode">user mode</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protection_ring#SUPERVISOR-MODE" title="Protection ring">kernel mode</a>. Programs and subsystems in user mode are limited in terms of what system resources they have access to, while the kernel mode has unrestricted access to the system memory and external devices. Kernel mode in Windows NT has full access to the hardware and system resources of the computer. The Windows NT <a href="/wiki/Kernel_(operating_system)" title="Kernel (operating system)">kernel</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_kernel" title="Hybrid kernel">hybrid kernel</a>; the architecture comprises a <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Windows_NT#Kernel" title="Architecture of Windows NT">simple kernel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hardware_abstraction_layer" class="mw-redirect" title="Hardware abstraction layer">hardware abstraction layer</a> (HAL), drivers, and a range of services (collectively named <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Windows_NT#Executive" title="Architecture of Windows NT">Executive</a>), which all exist in kernel mode.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Booting_process_of_Windows_NT" class="mw-redirect" title="Booting process of Windows NT">booting process of Windows NT</a> begins with <a href="/wiki/NTLDR" title="NTLDR">NTLDR</a> in versions before Vista and the <a href="/wiki/Windows_Boot_Manager" title="Windows Boot Manager">Windows Boot Manager</a> in Vista and later.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The boot loader is responsible for accessing the file system on the boot drive, starting <a href="/wiki/Ntoskrnl.exe" title="Ntoskrnl.exe">the kernel</a>, and loading boot-time device drivers into memory. Once all the boot and system drivers have been loaded, the kernel starts the <a href="/wiki/Session_Manager_Subsystem" title="Session Manager Subsystem">Session Manager Subsystem</a>. This process launches <a href="/wiki/Winlogon" title="Winlogon">winlogon</a>, which allows the user to login. Once the user is logged in <a href="/wiki/File_Explorer" title="File Explorer">File Explorer</a> is started, loading the <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">graphical user interface</a> of Windows NT.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Programming_language">Programming language</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Windows_NT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Programming language"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Windows NT is written in <a href="/wiki/C_(programming_language)" title="C (programming language)">C</a> and <a href="/wiki/C%2B%2B" title="C++">C++</a>, with a very small amount written in <a href="/wiki/Assembly_language" title="Assembly language">assembly language</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-TechNet-lesson2&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TechNet-lesson2-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> C is mostly used for the kernel code while C++ is mostly used for user-mode code. Assembly language is avoided where possible because it would impede <a href="/wiki/Software_portability" title="Software portability">portability</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Raymond-Chen-p10&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raymond-Chen-p10-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Windows NT 3.1 to 3.51 incorporated the <a href="/wiki/Program_Manager" title="Program Manager">Program Manager</a> and <a href="/wiki/Windows_File_Manager" title="Windows File Manager">File Manager</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Windows_3.1" title="Windows 3.1">Windows 3.1</a> series. Windows NT 4.0 onwards replaced those programs with <a href="/wiki/Windows_Explorer" class="mw-redirect" title="Windows Explorer">Windows Explorer</a> (including a <a href="/wiki/Taskbar" title="Taskbar">taskbar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Start_menu" title="Start menu">Start menu</a>), which originally appeared in <a href="/wiki/Windows_95" title="Windows 95">Windows 95</a>.
</p><p>The first release was given version number 3.1 to match the contemporary 16-bit Windows; magazines of that era claimed the number was also used to make that version seem more reliable than a ".0" release. Also the Novell IPX protocol was apparently licensed only to 3.1 versions of Windows software.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The NT version number is not now generally used for marketing purposes, but is still used internally, and said to reflect the degree of changes to the core of the operating system.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-msdnmag0112&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msdnmag0112-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, for application compatibility reasons, Microsoft kept the major version number as 6 in releases following Vista,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but changed it later to 10 in Windows 10.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-itprotoday-nt-10&#95;50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-itprotoday-nt-10-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The build number is an internal identifier used by Microsoft's developers and beta testers.
</p><p>Starting with <a href="/wiki/Windows_8.1" title="Windows 8.1">Windows 8.1</a>, Microsoft changed the Version API Helper functions' behavior. If an application is not manifested for Windows 8.1 or later, the API will always return version 6.2, which is the version number of <a href="/wiki/Windows_8" title="Windows 8">Windows 8</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is because the <i>manifest</i> feature was introduced with Windows 8.1,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to replace GetVersion and related functions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Supported_platforms">Supported platforms</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Windows_NT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Supported platforms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="32-bit_platforms">32-bit platforms</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Windows_NT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 32-bit platforms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In order to prevent <a href="/wiki/Intel_x86" class="mw-redirect" title="Intel x86">Intel x86</a>-specific code from slipping into the operating system, due to developers being used to developing on x86 chips, Windows NT 3.1 was initially developed using non-x86 development systems and then ported to the x86 architecture. This work was initially based on the <a href="/wiki/Intel_i860" title="Intel i860">Intel i860</a>-based <i>Dazzle</i> system and, later, the MIPS R3000-based <i><a href="/wiki/Jazz_(computer)" title="Jazz (computer)">Jazz</a></i> platform. Both systems were designed internally at Microsoft.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Windows NT 3.1 was released for Intel x86 <a href="/wiki/PC_compatible" class="mw-redirect" title="PC compatible">PC compatible</a> and <a href="/wiki/PC-98" title="PC-98">PC-98</a> platforms, and for <a href="/wiki/DEC_Alpha" title="DEC Alpha">DEC Alpha</a> and <a href="/wiki/ARC_(specification)" title="ARC (specification)">ARC</a>-compliant <a href="/wiki/MIPS_architecture" title="MIPS architecture">MIPS</a> platforms. Windows NT 3.51 added support for the <a href="/wiki/PowerPC" title="PowerPC">PowerPC</a> processor in 1995, specifically <a href="/wiki/PReP" class="mw-redirect" title="PReP">PReP</a>-compliant systems such as the <a href="/wiki/IBM_ThinkPad_Power_Series" class="mw-redirect" title="IBM ThinkPad Power Series">IBM ThinkPad Power Series</a> laptops and <a href="/wiki/Motorola" title="Motorola">Motorola</a> PowerStack series; but despite meetings between <a href="/wiki/Michael_Spindler" title="Michael Spindler">Michael Spindler</a> and Bill Gates, not on the <a href="/wiki/Power_Macintosh" title="Power Macintosh">Power Macintosh</a> as the PReP compliant Power Macintosh project failed to ship.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Intergraph_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Intergraph Corporation">Intergraph Corporation</a> ported Windows NT to its <a href="/wiki/Clipper_architecture" title="Clipper architecture">Clipper architecture</a> and later announced an intention to port Windows NT 3.51 to <a href="/wiki/Sun_Microsystems" title="Sun Microsystems">Sun Microsystems</a>' <a href="/wiki/SPARC" title="SPARC">SPARC</a> architecture,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in conjunction with the company's planned introduction of UltraSPARC models in 1995,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pcw199310&#95;windows&#95;nt&#95;64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pcw199310_windows_nt-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but neither version was sold to the public as a retail product.
</p><p>Only two of the Windows NT 4.0 variants (IA-32 and Alpha) have a full set of service packs available. All of the other ports done by third parties (Motorola, Intergraph, etc.) have few, if any, publicly available updates.
</p><p>Windows NT 4.0 was the last major release to support Alpha, MIPS, or PowerPC, though development of Windows 2000 for Alpha continued until August 1999, when <a href="/wiki/Compaq" title="Compaq">Compaq</a> stopped support for Windows NT on that architecture; and then three days later Microsoft also canceled their AlphaNT program,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> even though the Alpha NT 5 (Windows 2000) release had reached <a href="/wiki/Release_candidate" class="mw-redirect" title="Release candidate">RC1</a> status.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-alphant&#95;66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alphant-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On January 5, 2011, Microsoft announced that the next major version of the Windows NT family will include support for the <a href="/wiki/ARM_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="ARM architecture">ARM architecture</a>. Microsoft demonstrated a preliminary version of Windows (version 6.2.7867) running on an ARM-based computer at the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Electronics_Show" class="mw-redirect" title="Consumer Electronics Show">Consumer Electronics Show</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-windows-ces2011&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-windows-ces2011-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This eventually led to the commercial release of the <a href="/wiki/Windows_8" title="Windows 8">Windows 8</a>-derived <a href="/wiki/Windows_RT" title="Windows RT">Windows RT</a> on October 26, 2012, and the use of Windows NT, rather than Windows CE, in <a href="/wiki/Windows_Phone_8" title="Windows Phone 8">Windows Phone 8</a>.
</p><p>The original <a href="/wiki/Xbox_(console)" title="Xbox (console)">Xbox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xbox_360" title="Xbox 360">Xbox 360</a> run a custom <a href="/wiki/Operating_system" title="Operating system">operating system</a> based upon a heavily modified version of <a href="/wiki/Windows_2000" title="Windows 2000">Windows 2000</a>, an approach that Microsoft engineer Don Box called "fork and run".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-arstech&#95;windows&#95;everywhere&#95;68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arstech_windows_everywhere-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-leaked&#95;source&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leaked_source-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It exports <a href="/wiki/API" title="API">APIs</a> similar to those found in <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Direct3D" title="Direct3D">Direct3D</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Xbox_One" title="Xbox One">Xbox One</a> and <a href="/wiki/Xbox_Series_X/S" class="mw-redirect" title="Xbox Series X/S">Xbox Series X/S</a> consoles use a stripped-down version of the Windows operating system.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Windows 11 is the first non-server version of Windows NT that does not support 32-bit platforms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-reqs-11&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reqs-11-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-winminreq&#95;73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-winminreq-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="64-bit_platforms">64-bit platforms</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Windows_NT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 64-bit platforms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/64-bit" class="mw-redirect" title="64-bit">64-bit</a> versions of Windows NT were originally intended to run on <a href="/wiki/Itanium" title="Itanium">Itanium</a> and <a href="/wiki/DEC_Alpha" title="DEC Alpha">DEC Alpha</a>; the latter was used internally at Microsoft during early development of 64-bit Windows.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This continued for some time after Microsoft publicly announced that it was cancelling plans to ship 64-bit Windows for Alpha.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because of this, Alpha versions of Windows NT are 32-bit only.
</p><p>While <a href="/wiki/Windows_2000" title="Windows 2000">Windows 2000</a> only supports Intel <a href="/wiki/IA-32" title="IA-32">IA-32</a> (32-bit), Windows XP, Server 2003, Server 2008 and Server 2008 R2 each have one edition dedicated to Itanium-based systems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-IA-64-ed-2k3&#95;77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IA-64-ed-2k3-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In comparison with Itanium, Microsoft adopted <a href="/wiki/X64" class="mw-redirect" title="X64">x64</a> on a greater scale: every version of Windows since <a href="/wiki/Windows_XP" title="Windows XP">Windows XP</a> (which has a <a href="/wiki/Windows_XP_Professional_x64_Edition" title="Windows XP Professional x64 Edition">dedicated x64 edition</a>)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> has x64 editions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-IA-64-ed-2k3&#95;77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IA-64-ed-2k3-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-x64-ed-23k&#95;81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-x64-ed-23k-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The first version of Windows NT to support ARM64 devices with Qualcomm processors was <a href="/wiki/Windows_10,_version_1709" class="mw-redirect" title="Windows 10, version 1709">Windows 10, version 1709</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ARM64&#95;82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ARM64-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is a full version of Windows, rather than the cut-down <a href="/wiki/Windows_RT" title="Windows RT">Windows RT</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Hardware_requirements">Hardware requirements</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Windows_NT&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Hardware requirements"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The minimum hardware specification required to run each release of the professional workstation version of Windows NT has been fairly slow-moving until the 6.0 (Vista) release, which requires a minimum of 15 GB of free disk space, a tenfold increase in free disk space alone over the previous version, and the 2021 10.0 (11) release which excludes most systems built before 2018.
</p>
